                                                                Comments
                                                        Attach additional pages if necessary.
Dear Sir or Madam
I) Nature of Interest in the Project:
The following comments are submitted regarding the TransCanada Keystone Pipeline GP Ltd Project, with specific
reference to Section 9: Wildlife.
I have been a biologist for 22 years, and involved in impact assessment for nearly 20 of those years, and have been an
environmental scientist in Manitoba for the last 15 years. I live in the town of Starbuck, adjacent to the Project as it
 proceeds southward to the US border. My primary interest in the Project is with respect to the practice of
environmental assessment in Manitoba. I have focused my comments on four items with respect to wildlife issues the
in the assessment only. Terrestrial and aquatic wildlife is an area in which I have substantive experience in effects
assessment.
The following are my personal comments on the Project, independent of my employer, clients and any other
association of with I am a member.
II) Comments on the Application:
1) Determination of Significance: Section 9.7.1 “Definitions of Effects Significance” on pg 9-66 to 9-70. I note that the
standard or threshold of “significant effect” presented in Table 9-17 “Determination of Environmental Significance” is
such that the Project, by definition, cannot result in a “significant effect”. That is, during the Project’s existence or
“Medium Term Duration” even a “Moderate to High Magnitude” effect would still result in a finding of “Not
Significant”. Using this assessment criteria, there is no possible scenario whereby the Project, while it exists, could
result in a finding of “Significant Effect”, and all determinations of significance in Section 9: Wildlife should be
disregarded.
2) Manitoba Buffer Guidance: On pg 9-83, Table 9-22 “Buffer Considerations for Other Significant Resource Values in
Manitoba” is referenced to Manitoba Natural Resources (1996), however this citation is not provided in Section 9.10
“References”. I believe that the assessment is referring to the MDNR (1996) “Manitoba Natural Resources
Consolidated Buffer Management Guidelines”, but if this is the case the values presented in Table 9-22 are meant for
timber planning and operations and are not applicable outside the forested region of Manitoba. These guidelines are
not applicable to the Project.
3) Manitoba Endangered Species Act: Table 9-4 “Status of Wildlife in the Keystone Project Area” references the
Manitoba list to “MB Con (1993)”. In 1993, Manitoba Conservation did not exist, and the reference should have
reflected the departments name at the time of Manitoba Natural Resources. The list provided is also not applicable to
1993, but appears to use an updated list that includes species, which in 1993 were not yet included. The reference
should be to The Endangered Species Act – Threatened, Endangered and Extirpated Species Regulation. Table 9-4
also excludes the Peregrine Falcon and the Prairie Falcon from potentially occurring with the Project area in
Manitoba. The list also fails to recognize the potential migratory occurrence of both the Whooping Crane and Eskimo
Curlew, also listed by the Manitoba act. The potential presence of the listed threatened invertebrates the Dakota
Skipper and the Ottoe Skipper are also omitted. The subsequent assessment sections don’t specifically address the
potential effects of the Project in Manitoba on any of the species listed by the Act, and the potential for adverse effects
on any of these protected species in Manitoba is unknown.
4) The use of Professional judgment: Table 9-2 “Potential Effects on Species Diversity (VEC 1)” and Table 9-3
“Potential Effects on Wildlife Habitat Integrity (VEC 2)” appear to entirely justify the assessment conclusions based in
“Professional judgment, professional ESA experience”. This approach appears to circumvent the need to provide
detailed, logical, science-based justification for the assessment conclusions. As a result, it is difficult to review the
value of the assessment.
a. If this approach of justifying an assessment with “judgment” is acceptable;
i. How does the occurrence of minor errors in the assessment reflect upon the validity of the conclusions, not only the
examples given above, but others, like on pg 9-16, where the Project is described as occurring within the “Parkland
ecoregion and falls in the Winkler and Winnipeg ecodistricts” (the Project occurs within both the Aspen Parkland
Ecoregion, and also the Lake Manitoba Plain Ecoregion and extends across at least nine ecodistricts, as more
accurately reflected in the assessment Figures 9-10 and 9-11)?
ii. How does the occurrence of substantive omissions in the assessment reflect upon the validity of the conclusions if
they are based in judgment?
1. An example of this is the discussion of avian mortality associated with powerlines in Section 9.8.1.5 “Assessment of
Cumulative Effects” on pg 9-127. The section inappropriately discusses the potential for raptor electrocution as a
cumulative effect (the powerlines to the various stations are a necessary component of the Project and therefore are a
related physical activity, not a cumulative effect) but ignores the well-established effects described in the literature
regarding anticipated annual avian mortality due to collisions with the overhead wires (i.e. powerlines), which if
assumed to be four birds per kilometer of line per year, over the course of its life the Project would result the death of
hundreds to thousands of birds.
iii. How did the judgment address subtle complex issues like fragmentation that often have seemingly contradictory
effects depending on the circumstance?
1. For example; Table 9-30 “Assessment of Cumulative Effects on Valued Ecosystem Components” on pg 9-129
 presents in response to the effect “Increase in Habitat Fragmentation, Loss and Alteration” under “Mitigation
Measures” the concept of “Parallel pipeline with existing RoW whenever feasible”. The nature of this other “RoW” is
not discussed, therefore it is not known if it is another pipeline, a road, a highway, a powerline or a transmission line
or other feature or combination of features. In some cases this may be an effective mitigation, to concentrate the
effects of the Project in areas already affected by historical developments. In other cases, the cumulative effect of the
Project may be such that it causes an ecological threshold to be exceeded, resulting in a magnified effect. For
example, if the other RoWs are a highway, flanked by a transmission line RoW and a Powerline RoW, then the
cumulative effect of the addition of a fourth RoW may be enough to create an absolute barrier to movement for some
species. In this case, the separation of the Project’s RoW would be the best mitigation.
b. In Alberta, the Alberta Society of Professional Biologists exists, however no similar provincially recognized
organization exists in either Saskatchewan or Manitoba. The use of the term “Professional” may therefore have
different implications in the various distinctive provincial regions of the assessment with respect to the appropriate
use of professional judgment.
Concluding Remarks:
The Manitoba portions of the assessment do not adequately describe the potential effects on wildlife of the Project.
The assessment’s conclusions that the Project will not result in a significant adverse effect on Manitoba wildlife are
not supported.
Thank You
Don Harron B.Sc. P. Biol.

SUBJECT: TransCanada Keystone Pipeline
Manitoba Water Stewardship has reviewed the above noted proposal and submits the following
comments for your consideration:
Based on regional staff experience it is our preference that at minimum those crossings with a defined
channel and potential to carry water during spring runoff be directional drilled. This is due to the difficulty
in stabilizing those sites when open cut trenching is used and the ongoing erosion and sedimentation
which results.
A number of the “isolated” crossings would carry water during spring1 runoff. lf, as indicated by the
proposal these “isolated” and HDD crossings are done after June 15 and the mitigation proposed is
implemented, particularly the work is done in the dry, temporary and permanent erosion an sediment
control measures are implemented and the proponent is willing to monitor and rectify any issues, then our
concerns should be addressed.
Any fish salvage operations will require a Live Fish Handling Permit issued by Fisheries Branch, Manitoba
Water Stewardship. We would like to see any site specific vehicle crossings implemented according to
the Manitoba Stream Crossing Guidelines for the Protection of Fish.
Our comments, with the exception of the permitting process, do not take precedent over DFO’S review.
As long as they are involved in reviewing this proposal and manage fish habitat to meet the intent of their
no net loss policy, provincial fisheries management interests should be met.
A permit from Manitoba Water Stewardship is required for any drainage works undertaken on any lands
where it is proposed to alter or divert the natural flow of a watercourse. This includes the placement of
culverts, dykes, dams, and drainage or consolidation of any type of wetland.
According to section 10.2, different study areas of variable distances on either side of the Right of Way
were considered. Subsequently, section 10.3 suggested some scenarios related to groundwater, What
will be the precautions?
l) Where drinking water pipelines are located within the above study areas?
it) When an oil pipeline crosses a water main?
As with alt pipeline projects, hydrostatic testing of a pipeline requires an authortzation under The Water
Rights Act.
Please be advised that the proposed pipe‘iine crosses various Provincial Waterways and therefore
requires authorization from Manitoba Water Stewardship under The Water Resources Administration Act.
Manitoba Water Stewardship will check the location map of the subject project and notify you about the
terms and conditions of the required authorization in due course. Should you have any questions, please
contact Paul Dhaliwal at 2049456995.

PROJECT DESCRIPTION
The prop sed Keystone pipeline would extend ﬁom Hardisty, Alberta to Wood River and
Patoka, Il inois in the United States of America. The Canadian portion of the proj ect would run
' from Ha: isty to a point near Haskell; Manitoba at the Intemational Border.
The pipel e would include two parts: conversion of approximately 864 kilometres of an existing
natural 3 ,. Pipeline m S&SkﬁtCheWﬁﬂ and. Manitoba to permit the transmissionof oil and . - -~ --- ~ ~-
' "' " ' ' "consume )3 of approximately 371 kilometres of new oil pipeline in Alberta (268 km),
Saskatche an (3 km), and Manitoba (100 km). Pipeline construction will require a 30 metre
wide righ of-way (ROW), consisting of 20 metres of pennanent ROW and approximately 10
metres of emporary workspace.
The porti n of the proj eat that involves the conversion from natural gas transmission to oil would
also requi e the commotion of 9 pump stations and would include 19 electric pumping units.
The porti m of the project involving the construction ofnew pipeline in Alberta and
Saskatche an would include 5 pump stations With 15 electric pumping units. The portion of the
proj eat in >olving the construction of new pipeline in Manitoba would include 2 pump stations
with 5 ele tric pumping uhits. Pump stations will require 1.5 ha of land each including
tempor workspace. The stations will have pump pads equipped With drip pads that will be
drained in 0 an undergound sump. The sumps would be double-walled tanks that would be
monitore- as part of Keystone’s proposed pipeline monitoring system.
The proj t would also include construction of 3 operational tanks with a total volume of about
1,050,000 bbl, associated manifold piping, and control and metering facilities at Hardisty,
Alberta I he footprint for this component of the proposal is estimated at 13 ha.
ATMOSP ERIC ENVIRONMENT

greenhous gases (GHGS) from construction and fugitive emissions of H28, volatile organic
compounos (VOCS) and meroaptans ﬁrom operation of the three oil product storage tanks.

PM, THC: and GHG emissions. Consfmctien emissiens were quantiﬁed in the environmental
impact statement (EIS) and are expected to be short-tenn and transient in nature. The Alberta.—
Saskatche an and Manitoba portions of the pipeline are estimated by the proponent to take 90
deys and 100 days for construction completion respectively. Construction of the storage tanks
wﬂl take 1 55 time. Construction of the pump stations is expected te take approximately one

environm- tal assessments of new development proj acts. The policy promotes the maintenance
, ,. , ,ofthe fun. tions and, valuasderived .fmmwetlands thoughout Canada, enhancement. and. . . . .. .. . .. .
rehabilita ion of wetlands in areas where continuing loss or degradation of wetlands have
' reached c ‘tical levels, and utilization of wetlands in a mmmer that enhances prospects for their
sustained v d productive use by ﬁxture generations.

Sggiact: Tranecanada Keystone Pigeline GP Ltd. -- Agglicaﬂan fer Keystone Pigeline

Transpert Canada has reviewed the applicatien for the Keystone Pipeline submitted to the National Energy Board on
December 12, 2006. .

Transport Canada's integest in the Praieet

Transport Canada issues Navigabie Waters Protection Act (NWPA) approvals for watercourse crossing across navigabie .
watercourses. NWPA Approvals issued under Section 5(1) or Sectien 6(4) of the NWPA require that an environmental
assessment be conducted under the Canadian En vironmental Assessment Act (CEAA). The proponent has identified 16
watercourse crossings in Alberta and 40 watercourse crossings in Maniteba that comprise part of the project. Transport
Canada’s NWP office has reviewed the information previded on each of the watercourse crossings and, based on the
information provided to date, has been unable to determine whether or not NWP approvals are required for these
watercourse crossings or which type of NWP approve! may he issued for any crossing. Additionally, NWP applications for
approvals may be required for any vehicle or equipment cressings over navigable waterways. Accordingiy, Transport
Canada has advised the proponent to submit NWP applications for each crossing to Transport Canada’s NWP ofﬁce. The
NWP ofﬁce will review these applications and advise which types at NWP approval will be required for each crossing.
Trans: art 031 adg ’..!ﬂ............m..........ti the .___.._...._.........E ms

Until such time as Transport Canada's Navigabie Water Protection Office receives applications for approval under the
NWPA and can advise of the type of the approvals that may he issued fer the project, Transport Canada's Environmental
Affairs is proceeding under the assumption that there may be CEAA triggers (Le. issuance of an NWP Section 5(1)
approval) for any at the watercourse crossings associated with the project. Accordingly, Transport Canada is participating
in the cnordinated federal environmental assessment review of the project to fulfill its duties as a Responsible Authority
under CEAA.

Transport Canada intends to limit its involvement in the environmental assessment process to those components of the
project for which it has a CEAA trigger (Le, watercourse crossings of navigabie watercourses requiring an NWP Section
5(1) Approvai). Accerdingly, Transport Canada intends to restrict its input into the NEE prepared screening report for the
project to watercourse cressings cf navigebie watercourses for which ii: may issue a Section 5(1) or Section 6(4) approval
under the NWPA.

Other Matters

in Section i3: Other Autherizatiehs, Appendix “i3-‘i, Tabie “3331-1 centains a reference to a “permit for exception“ under the
Transportation of Dangerous Goods Act (TDGA). Please be advised that the prcper wording is Permit for equivalent level
of safety (PELS).
If TransCanada or one of their subcontractors pians to conduct an activity which is outside what the TDG reguiations allow,
they must make eppiicatien ta Transport Cenacia to conduct such werk and demonstrate that what they pian ta do is safe
and wouid provide an “equivaient ievei of safety” as foiiewing the TDG regulations.

f? HPR"12*2007 THU 03:45 PM Transport Canada Program FAX N0. 2049835048 P. 03
The onus is on the company conducting this work to make an appiication for a PELS well in advance of any activity that
requires this parmit for relief from any TDG reguiatcry requirement.

3. On November 28, 2006, Curt Helm, WEB Water Development Association General
Manager in North Dakota, wrote with several cenaems regarding the impacts of the
Keystone Pipeline on the rural water system and the rural community served by WEB.
The concerns include that in the event of a spill or leak, the PVC waterlines could be

' breached end the water supply contaminated. The letter suggests that the oil pipeline be
encased in a second steel pipe for 300 ft on either side of the water line were crossed. The
letter also states that the EIS should address the emergency response measures in the
event of a leak or spill. WEB recommends that emergency response personnel be
stationed at intervals of not less than 70 to 90 miles. The EIS should also address the
effects of a Spill or leak on the J ames River basin. Before a permit is issued a sample of
the crude oils should be taken and analyzed to determinepotential impacts en PVC pipe,
soils, water, air quality and the environment. Other issues discussed in the letter include
